Hong Kong: Agency (1940-1950)

Christian or Pro-Christian Leaders

  1. Wong Oi-tong (ÁéãÊÑõÊ£†, 1881‚Äì1957) (Cited from Li Zhigang, 2014: 31-35)

    Wong Oi-tong, born in Dongguan, Guangdong, was a third-generation Christian. His grandfather, Wong Chun-kwong, and father, Wong Siu-tuen, were both members and preachers of the Rhenish Mission. Wong Oi-tong studied at the Rhenish Primary School in Fuyong, Bao‚Äôan County, before attending Rhenish Secondary School in Yeshan, Tangxia, Dongguan. At the age of 20, he entered the Rhenish Theological Seminary in Dongguan and graduated at 22. The following year, he was ordained as a preacher for the Rhenish Mission. After arriving in Hong Kong in 1909, he remained there permanently.

    In 1914, when the Rhenish Church in Hong Kong was established, Wong assisted Pastor Chan Koon-hai in managing church affairs. During World War I, due to hostilities between Britain and Germany, German missionaries in Hong Kong were ordered to leave. As the Rhenish Mission had German roots, it was placed under the custody of the London Missionary Society. Wong drafted and established the church‚Äôs regulations during this period. In 1917, he was ordained as a pastor in Humen. By 1925, after the London Missionary Society completed its custodianship, the Rhenish Mission achieved self-governance. In 1926, Pastor Yip To-sheng arrived to take over church administration, and Wong was elected Vice President of the General Assembly.

    Wong was also a founding member of the Hong Kong Christian Council in 1914, serving as its first secretary and drafting its constitution. He held various roles, such as head of the Council‚Äôs Evangelism and Public Cemetery Departments. Wong actively opposed the practice of keeping slave girls, publishing articles in The Great Light Newspaper to condemn this custom. During the Japanese occupation of Hong Kong in 1941, Wong was appointed President of the Japanese-controlled Hong Kong Christian Council due to the Rhenish Mission‚Äôs German background. After the war, the Council was disbanded, and its departments resumed their activities. Wong suffered a stroke in December 1948, leaving him paralyzed, and he passed away in May 1957.

    ‚Äî

    1. Moritaka Samejima (ÈÆ´Â≥∂ÁõõÈöÜ, 1897‚Äì1986)

      (Cited from Chen Zhiheng, 2009: 234-246; Samejima Moritaka, 1971)

      Moritaka Samejima was born in December 1897 in Sasebo, Nagasaki. After graduating from the Kansai Gakuin Theological Department in 1927, he pursued further studies at Emory University and the San Francisco Theological Seminary. In 1931, he was ordained as a pastor at the Kobe Hirano Methodist Church and became the district superintendent of the Korean Methodist Church in Seoul in 1934. In 1941, he was appointed Chaplain (later Religious Director) at Kansai University.

      In September 1943, Samejima was summoned to Hong Kong to serve as an advisor to the Japanese-controlled ‚ÄúHong Kong Christian Council,‚Äù a position he held until the war‚Äôs end. His primary duty was to act as a liaison between the Japanese governor‚Äôs office and the Christian churches. For instance, upon learning that the Japanese military intended to remove the stained-glass windows of St. John‚Äôs Cathedral, Samejima intervened to ensure their preservation. He also opposed the conversion of churches into stables and worked to secure the release of Reverend Chang Chu-ling, who was falsely accused of espionage by the Japanese military.

      After Japan‚Äôs surrender, Samejima was interned and later repatriated to Japan. In 1946, he returned to Kansai Gakuin as Dean of Student Affairs and served as the eighth Chaplain from November 1946 to June

  1. From 1951 to 1974, he was the President of Chinzei Gakuin. Samejima revisited Hong Kong in 1969, where he was warmly received by the local church community. In 1970, he published Recollections of Hong Kong: The Church Under Occupation, which was translated into Chinese the following year. He passed away in February 1986.

    ‚Äî

    1. Yan-Tak Woo (ËÉ°ÊÅ©Âæ∑, 1907‚Äì2005)

      (Cited from Xing Fuzeng, 2014a: 203-206)

      Yan-Tak Woo was born in May 1907 into a prominent Christian family in Hong Kong. His grandfather, Woo Him-hang (1830‚Äì1889), was a pastor in the Berlin Mission, while his father, Woo Yee-tung (1884‚Äì1948), served as a deacon at St. Stephen‚Äôs Church under the Anglican Church. Yan-Tak Woo was the second of eight children. He was baptized at St. Stephen‚Äôs Church at birth. His mother, Wong Pik-lin (1885‚Äì1965), was the seventh daughter of Pastor Wong Chim-yu (1847‚Äì1907), a minister in the Rhenish Mission and a member of the Chinese Evangelistic Society founded by Karl G√ºtzlaff.

      Woo attended Ying Wa Girls‚Äô School and a sports school in Central before enrolling at Diocesan Boys‚Äô School. In 1926, while preparing for the University of Hong Kong‚Äôs entrance exam, he was diagnosed with tuberculosis and moved to his cousin‚Äôs clinic in Aberdeen to recover. During this period, he attended the first Believers‚Äô Bible Conference in 1927, where he was deeply influenced by Pastor Wong Yuen-so (Christian and Missionary Alliance in Guangxi). Woo experienced a profound spiritual awakening, leading to his active ministry.

      From 1930 to 1935, Woo assumed full-time ministry responsibilities at the Joyful Church, initially established by Julia Meadows, a faith missionary. He continued leading the church through its relocations and name changes, eventually settling in Tai Po Road under the name Joyful Gospel Church by 1937.

      During the Japanese occupation of Hong Kong in 1941, Woo refused to join the Japanese-controlled Christian Council, citing ‚Äúdifferences in faith.‚Äù As a result, he faced pressure and was briefly detained by the Japanese military police on suspicions of collaborating with Western powers.

      After the war, Woo was invited to lead revival meetings across China and became a prominent preacher, especially influential in student fellowship movements. His ministry and the Joyful Gospel Church became significant references for the evangelical movement in Hong Kong. Woo later served as the principal of seminaries in Singapore, the Philippines, and Hong Kong. He authored numerous works, including Running Toward the Goal, Table Devotions, and The Christian and the Sabbath. Woo passed away in 2005 after a long illness.

      1. John So (Ëòá‰ΩêÊèö, 1916‚Äì2007)

        (Cited from Li Jinqiang, 2014: 211-213)

        John So was born in 1916 in Hong Kong to a Christian family from Yangjiang County, Guangdong Province. A third-generation Christian, So was passionate about music and literature from an early age, composing over 600 Hymns of Heaven and Earth and more than 200 Sacred Songs of Heaven and Earth throughout his life. In 1931, he committed to serving God after attending a revival meeting in Kowloon led by Chinese evangelist Song Shangjie (John Sung). In 1934, So went to the North China Theological Seminary in Teng County, Shandong Province, for theological education. After graduating in 1937, he engaged in rural evangelism in Zhangjiakou, Chahar, and Shanxi before returning to Hong Kong.

        In 1940, So was invited to assist in editing a Hebrew dictionary and teaching music at the North China Theological Seminary. In 1941, Pastor Yang Shaotang invited him to teach Hebrew in Tianjin‚Äôs Spiritual Work League. Later, he returned to Hong Kong to teach biblical studies at the Bethel Theological Seminary, which had relocated from Shanghai. During the Japanese occupation of Hong Kong, So fled to Zhangzhou, Fujian, where he founded the Heaven and Earth Daily. In 1943, he joined the faculty at Guizhou Bethel Theological Seminary, where he also launched The Gospel Magazine for the Miao Christian community. He later taught at the China Spiritual Theology Seminary in Chongqing, working alongside Pastor Jia Yuming.

        In 1945, So served as the General Secretary of the ‚ÄúGansu-Ningxia-Qinghai Christian Federation,‚Äù promoting inter-church initiatives in Northwest China. He was ordained as a pastor in Gansu in 1947 and later worked in Shanghai with the China Inland Mission on literature ministry before returning to Hong Kong in 1948.

        Between 1953 and 1961, So embarked on an eight-year evangelistic journey across East Asia, preaching in 13 countries, including Japan, Singapore, Malaysia, Australia, and Indonesia. From 1962 to 1976, he undertook global evangelistic tours across six continents and more than 50 countries. In 1976, after returning from the Americas, he became president of the ‚ÄúGlobal Evangelistic Association‚Äù and founded the ‚ÄúChristian Heaven and Earth Society,‚Äù publishing over 50 books, including Bible Difficulties (10 volumes), New Testament Meditations (8 volumes), and Old Testament Insights (3 volumes).

        He passed away in September 2007 at the age of 91.

        ‚Äî

        1. John K. C. Ma (È¶¨Êï¨ÂÖ®, 1917‚Äì2011)

          (Cited from Congregational Church, 2012; Peng Shumin, 2014: 92-95)

          John K. C. Ma was born in 1917 at the site of the Hong Kong Congregational Church. As a teenager, he accompanied his father, Pastor Ma Yaodong, in street evangelism, playing the accordion and leading hymns. After graduating from Wah Yan College in 1935, he worked at the Salvation Army in Guangzhou. During World War II, he served as a member of the International Relief Council. In 1944, Ma graduated from the Union Theological Seminary in Guangzhou and became the pastor of the Qujiang Chapel of the Chinese YMCA in 1945. The following year, he was appointed Youth Secretary of the Guangdong Association of the Chinese Christian Church and was ordained as a pastor at Renji Chapel in Guangzhou in 1946.

          In 1947, Ma attended the World Christian Youth Conference in Oslo, where he reaffirmed his lifelong call to ministry. After the conference, during a stopover in Hong Kong, he learned of the Congregational Church‚Äôs urgent need for leadership. In September 1947, Ma decided to stay and became the second senior pastor of the Congregational Church, serving for 40 years until his retirement in 1987, except for a year of advanced study at Union Theological Seminary in New York (1956‚Äì1957).

          During his tenure, Ma initiated the construction of new church facilities. To fund the Leighton Road Chapel, he proposed issuing bonds with a 5% annual interest, a plan that successfully raised the required funds within a year. The chapel, initially called ‚ÄúCaroline Hill Road Chapel,‚Äù was dedicated in 1950. Later, Ma led the redevelopment of the Ladder Street church building, completed in 1971 and renamed ‚ÄúBridges Street Chapel.‚Äù

          Ma also taught at theological institutions, including the Hong Kong Theological Seminary, Chung Chi Divinity School, and the Lutheran Theological Seminary. He served as chairman of the Hong Kong Council of the Church of Christ in China and as vice-chairman of the Hong Kong Chinese Christian Churches Union. Ma suggested limiting the union board members‚Äô terms to six years with a mandatory one-year break after.

          He passed away in April 2011 at the age of 94.

          ‚Äî

          1. Denial Cheung (ÂºµÊúâÂÖâ, 1917‚Äì1996)

            (Cited from Wong Cailian, 2014: 42-46)

            Denial Cheung was born in 1917 in Gaoming, Guangdong Province, into a pastor‚Äôs family. His father, Cheung Shing-yau, was a minister of the Holiness Church. In 1927, after moving to Hong Kong, Cheung was baptized at the Kowloon Union Church and became active in Sunday School, youth fellowship, and the choir. He experienced a spiritual revival during a John Sung evangelistic campaign in Hong Kong while in high school. After graduating from Munsang College in 1937, Cheung worked at the Far Eastern Missionary Society‚Äôs seminary in Guangzhou as an English translator and music teacher.

            In 1940, Cheung entered Bethel Theological Seminary, which had relocated to Hong Kong from Shanghai. During the Japanese occupation, he followed the seminary to Guangxi and Guizhou, graduating in 1943. He ministered in Guizhou and joined evangelist Ji Zhiwen on preaching tours.

            After the war, Cheung moved back to Hong Kong and served as a religious officer for the YMCA. Later, he worked at Kowloon City Baptist Church, initially as a volunteer director of youth and choir ministries, before being ordained as a pastor in 1949. He pursued advanced studies in religious education at Southwestern Baptist Theological Seminary in the U.S. in 1952 and returned to Kowloon City Baptist Church in 1954. He led significant reforms in Sunday School, doubling participation between 1954 and 1959.

            In 1959, Cheung emigrated to the U.S., working with the Chinese Consolidated Benevolent Association in Chicago. He earned a master‚Äôs degree in religious education in 1965 and returned to Hong Kong later that year, resuming his role at Kowloon City Baptist Church. Cheung served as chairman or president of various organizations, including the Hong Kong Chinese Christian Churches Union, the Hong Kong Baptist Convention, and the Baptist World Alliance. He chaired the Billy Graham Crusades in Hong Kong in 1956 and 1976.

            Cheung retired in 1980 and passed away in 1996 at the age of 79.

                1. Wong Oi-tong (ÁéãÊÑõÊ£†, 1881‚Äì1957) (Cited from Li Zhigang, 2014: 31-35)

                  Wong Oi-tong, born in Dongguan, Guangdong, was a third-generation Christian. His grandfather, Wong Chun-kwong, and father, Wong Siu-tuen, were both members and preachers of the Rhenish Mission. Wong Oi-tong studied at the Rhenish Primary School in Fuyong, Bao‚Äôan County, before attending Rhenish Secondary School in Yeshan, Tangxia, Dongguan. At the age of 20, he entered the Rhenish Theological Seminary in Dongguan and graduated at 22. The following year, he was ordained as a preacher for the Rhenish Mission. After arriving in Hong Kong in 1909, he remained there permanently.

                  In 1914, when the Rhenish Church in Hong Kong was established, Wong assisted Pastor Chan Koon-hai in managing church affairs. During World War I, due to hostilities between Britain and Germany, German missionaries in Hong Kong were ordered to leave. As the Rhenish Mission had German roots, it was placed under the custody of the London Missionary Society. Wong drafted and established the church‚Äôs regulations during this period. In 1917, he was ordained as a pastor in Humen. By 1925, after the London Missionary Society completed its custodianship, the Rhenish Mission achieved self-governance. In 1926, Pastor Yip To-sheng arrived to take over church administration, and Wong was elected Vice President of the General Assembly.

                  Wong was also a founding member of the Hong Kong Christian Council in 1914, serving as its first secretary and drafting its constitution. He held various roles, such as head of the Council‚Äôs Evangelism and Public Cemetery Departments. Wong actively opposed the practice of keeping slave girls, publishing articles in The Great Light Newspaper to condemn this custom. During the Japanese occupation of Hong Kong in 1941, Wong was appointed President of the Japanese-controlled Hong Kong Christian Council due to the Rhenish Mission‚Äôs German background. After the war, the Council was disbanded, and its departments resumed their activities. Wong suffered a stroke in December 1948, leaving him paralyzed, and he passed away in May 1957.

                  ‚Äî

                  1. Moritaka Samejima (ÈÆ´Â≥∂ÁõõÈöÜ, 1897‚Äì1986)

                    (Cited from Chen Zhiheng, 2009: 234-246; Samejima Moritaka, 1971)

                    Moritaka Samejima was born in December 1897 in Sasebo, Nagasaki. After graduating from the Kansai Gakuin Theological Department in 1927, he pursued further studies at Emory University and the San Francisco Theological Seminary. In 1931, he was ordained as a pastor at the Kobe Hirano Methodist Church and became the district superintendent of the Korean Methodist Church in Seoul in 1934. In 1941, he was appointed Chaplain (later Religious Director) at Kansai University.

                    In September 1943, Samejima was summoned to Hong Kong to serve as an advisor to the Japanese-controlled ‚ÄúHong Kong Christian Council,‚Äù a position he held until the war‚Äôs end. His primary duty was to act as a liaison between the Japanese governor‚Äôs office and the Christian churches. For instance, upon learning that the Japanese military intended to remove the stained-glass windows of St. John‚Äôs Cathedral, Samejima intervened to ensure their preservation. He also opposed the conversion of churches into stables and worked to secure the release of Reverend Chang Chu-ling, who was falsely accused of espionage by the Japanese military.

                    After Japan‚Äôs surrender, Samejima was interned and later repatriated to Japan. In 1946, he returned to Kansai Gakuin as Dean of Student Affairs and served as the eighth Chaplain from November 1946 to June

  2. From 1951 to 1974, he was the President of Chinzei Gakuin. Samejima revisited Hong Kong in 1969, where he was warmly received by the local church community. In 1970, he published Recollections of Hong Kong: The Church Under Occupation, which was translated into Chinese the following year. He passed away in February 1986.

    1. Yan-Tak Woo (ËÉ°ÊÅ©Âæ∑, 1907‚Äì2005)

      (Cited from Xing Fuzeng, 2014a: 203-206)

      Yan-Tak Woo was born in May 1907 into a prominent Christian family in Hong Kong. His grandfather, Woo Him-hang (1830‚Äì1889), was a pastor in the Berlin Mission, while his father, Woo Yee-tung (1884‚Äì1948), served as a deacon at St. Stephen‚Äôs Church under the Anglican Church. Yan-Tak Woo was the second of eight children. He was baptized at St. Stephen‚Äôs Church at birth. His mother, Wong Pik-lin (1885‚Äì1965), was the seventh daughter of Pastor Wong Chim-yu (1847‚Äì1907), a minister in the Rhenish Mission and a member of the Chinese Evangelistic Society founded by Karl G√ºtzlaff.

      Woo attended Ying Wa Girls‚Äô School and a sports school in Central before enrolling at Diocesan Boys‚Äô School. In 1926, while preparing for the University of Hong Kong‚Äôs entrance exam, he was diagnosed with tuberculosis and moved to his cousin‚Äôs clinic in Aberdeen to recover. During this period, he attended the first Believers‚Äô Bible Conference in 1927, where he was deeply influenced by Pastor Wong Yuen-so (Christian and Missionary Alliance in Guangxi). Woo experienced a profound spiritual awakening, leading to his active ministry.

      From 1930 to 1935, Woo assumed full-time ministry responsibilities at the Joyful Church, initially established by Julia Meadows, a faith missionary. He continued leading the church through its relocations and name changes, eventually settling in Tai Po Road under the name Joyful Gospel Church by 1937.

      During the Japanese occupation of Hong Kong in 1941, Woo refused to join the Japanese-controlled Christian Council, citing ‚Äúdifferences in faith.‚Äù As a result, he faced pressure and was briefly detained by the Japanese military police on suspicions of collaborating with Western powers.

      After the war, Woo was invited to lead revival meetings across China and became a prominent preacher, especially influential in student fellowship movements. His ministry and the Joyful Gospel Church became significant references for the evangelical movement in Hong Kong. Woo later served as the principal of seminaries in Singapore, the Philippines, and Hong Kong. He authored numerous works, including Running Toward the Goal, Table Devotions, and The Christian and the Sabbath. Woo passed away in 2005 after a long illness.

      1. John So (Ëòá‰ΩêÊèö, 1916‚Äì2007)

        (Cited from Li Jinqiang, 2014: 211-213)

        John So was born in 1916 in Hong Kong to a Christian family from Yangjiang County, Guangdong Province. A third-generation Christian, So was passionate about music and literature from an early age, composing over 600 Hymns of Heaven and Earth and more than 200 Sacred Songs of Heaven and Earth throughout his life. In 1931, he committed to serving God after attending a revival meeting in Kowloon led by Chinese evangelist Song Shangjie (John Sung). In 1934, So went to the North China Theological Seminary in Teng County, Shandong Province, for theological education. After graduating in 1937, he engaged in rural evangelism in Zhangjiakou, Chahar, and Shanxi before returning to Hong Kong.

        In 1940, So was invited to assist in editing a Hebrew dictionary and teaching music at the North China Theological Seminary. In 1941, Pastor Yang Shaotang invited him to teach Hebrew in Tianjin‚Äôs Spiritual Work League. Later, he returned to Hong Kong to teach biblical studies at the Bethel Theological Seminary, which had relocated from Shanghai. During the Japanese occupation of Hong Kong, So fled to Zhangzhou, Fujian, where he founded the Heaven and Earth Daily. In 1943, he joined the faculty at Guizhou Bethel Theological Seminary, where he also launched The Gospel Magazine for the Miao Christian community. He later taught at the China Spiritual Theology Seminary in Chongqing, working alongside Pastor Jia Yuming.

        In 1945, So served as the General Secretary of the ‚ÄúGansu-Ningxia-Qinghai Christian Federation,‚Äù promoting inter-church initiatives in Northwest China. He was ordained as a pastor in Gansu in 1947 and later worked in Shanghai with the China Inland Mission on literature ministry before returning to Hong Kong in 1948.

        Between 1953 and 1961, So embarked on an eight-year evangelistic journey across East Asia, preaching in 13 countries, including Japan, Singapore, Malaysia, Australia, and Indonesia. From 1962 to 1976, he undertook global evangelistic tours across six continents and more than 50 countries. In 1976, after returning from the Americas, he became president of the ‚ÄúGlobal Evangelistic Association‚Äù and founded the ‚ÄúChristian Heaven and Earth Society,‚Äù publishing over 50 books, including Bible Difficulties (10 volumes), New Testament Meditations (8 volumes), and Old Testament Insights (3 volumes).

        He passed away in September 2007 at the age of 91.

        ‚Äî

        1. John K. C. Ma (È¶¨Êï¨ÂÖ®, 1917‚Äì2011)

          (Cited from Congregational Church, 2012; Peng Shumin, 2014: 92-95)

          John K. C. Ma was born in 1917 at the site of the Hong Kong Congregational Church. As a teenager, he accompanied his father, Pastor Ma Yaodong, in street evangelism, playing the accordion and leading hymns. After graduating from Wah Yan College in 1935, he worked at the Salvation Army in Guangzhou. During World War II, he served as a member of the International Relief Council. In 1944, Ma graduated from the Union Theological Seminary in Guangzhou and became the pastor of the Qujiang Chapel of the Chinese YMCA in 1945. The following year, he was appointed Youth Secretary of the Guangdong Association of the Chinese Christian Church and was ordained as a pastor at Renji Chapel in Guangzhou in 1946.

          In 1947, Ma attended the World Christian Youth Conference in Oslo, where he reaffirmed his lifelong call to ministry. After the conference, during a stopover in Hong Kong, he learned of the Congregational Church‚Äôs urgent need for leadership. In September 1947, Ma decided to stay and became the second senior pastor of the Congregational Church, serving for 40 years until his retirement in 1987, except for a year of advanced study at Union Theological Seminary in New York (1956‚Äì1957).

          During his tenure, Ma initiated the construction of new church facilities. To fund the Leighton Road Chapel, he proposed issuing bonds with a 5% annual interest, a plan that successfully raised the required funds within a year. The chapel, initially called ‚ÄúCaroline Hill Road Chapel,‚Äù was dedicated in 1950. Later, Ma led the redevelopment of the Ladder Street church building, completed in 1971 and renamed ‚ÄúBridges Street Chapel.‚Äù

          Ma also taught at theological institutions, including the Hong Kong Theological Seminary, Chung Chi Divinity School, and the Lutheran Theological Seminary. He served as chairman of the Hong Kong Council of the Church of Christ in China and as vice-chairman of the Hong Kong Chinese Christian Churches Union. Ma suggested limiting the union board members‚Äô terms to six years with a mandatory one-year break after.

          He passed away in April 2011 at the age of 94.

          ‚Äî

          1. Denial Cheung (ÂºµÊúâÂÖâ, 1917‚Äì1996)

            (Cited from Wong Cailian, 2014: 42-46)

            Denial Cheung was born in 1917 in Gaoming, Guangdong Province, into a pastor‚Äôs family. His father, Cheung Shing-yau, was a minister of the Holiness Church. In 1927, after moving to Hong Kong, Cheung was baptized at the Kowloon Union Church and became active in Sunday School, youth fellowship, and the choir. He experienced a spiritual revival during a John Sung evangelistic campaign in Hong Kong while in high school. After graduating from Munsang College in 1937, Cheung worked at the Far Eastern Missionary Society‚Äôs seminary in Guangzhou as an English translator and music teacher.

            In 1940, Cheung entered Bethel Theological Seminary, which had relocated to Hong Kong from Shanghai. During the Japanese occupation, he followed the seminary to Guangxi and Guizhou, graduating in 1943. He ministered in Guizhou and joined evangelist Ji Zhiwen on preaching tours.

            After the war, Cheung moved back to Hong Kong and served as a religious officer for the YMCA. Later, he worked at Kowloon City Baptist Church, initially as a volunteer director of youth and choir ministries, before being ordained as a pastor in 1949. He pursued advanced studies in religious education at Southwestern Baptist Theological Seminary in the U.S. in 1952 and returned to Kowloon City Baptist Church in 1954. He led significant reforms in Sunday School, doubling participation between 1954 and 1959.

            In 1959, Cheung emigrated to the U.S., working with the Chinese Consolidated Benevolent Association in Chicago. He earned a master‚Äôs degree in religious education in 1965 and returned to Hong Kong later that year, resuming his role at Kowloon City Baptist Church. Cheung served as chairman or president of various organizations, including the Hong Kong Chinese Christian Churches Union, the Hong Kong Baptist Convention, and the Baptist World Alliance. He chaired the Billy Graham Crusades in Hong Kong in 1956 and 1976.

            Cheung retired in 1980 and passed away in 1996 at the age of 79.

            1. Christian Population in Hong Kong (1940)

              (Cited from Liu Yue Sheng, 1996: 350)

              The Hong Kong Blue Book ceased publication in 1941, but its final issue in 1940 included ecclesiastical statistics. The only recorded data on Hong Kong‚Äôs Christian population comes from Liu Yue Sheng‚Äôs History of Christianity in Hong Kong, which stated that in 1940, there were 43 churches in Hong Kong with a total of 28,808 Christians. This figure includes expatriates, but the number of Chinese Christians was 28,743 after excluding expatriates. This starkly contrasts with the 6,220 worshippers reported in 1939 by the Blue Book. The discrepancy likely stems from the 1940 figure being based on church membership records, while the 1939 figure was based on worship attendance. Moreover, the Blue Book only recorded mainstream denominations (e.g., Anglican, London Missionary Society, Congregational, Methodist, Basel, and Rhenish Mission) and excluded other Chinese churches.

              ‚Äî

              1. Chapel of the Christian & Missionary Alliance, Kowloon Tong, 1940

                (Cited from He Ming Zhang, 2021: 35-41)

                The Christian and Missionary Alliance (C&MA) initially had no plans to establish a church in Hong Kong. However, in 1933, the Alliance Children Hostel was established under the care of John Bechtel and his wife to accommodate the children of missionaries stationed in South China. In December 1940, Pastor Bechtel rented a garage at 25 Cumberland Road, Kowloon Tong, for worship services for missionary children and outreach to the Chinese. It was named the ‚ÄúHong Kong Branch Chapel of the Guangxi Christian and Missionary Alliance.‚Äù

                During the Japanese occupation, Bechtel was imprisoned in a concentration camp, and church activities halted. Two Christians, Xu Ze Lin and Zheng Xi Shou, received permission from the Japanese commander to reopen the worship venue. This drew Christians of various denominations in Kowloon Tong. Attendance increased, and by the summer of 1942, services were held at a nearby kindergarten.

                After the war, Pastor Bechtel returned and prioritized the church‚Äôs self-governance. In 1947, land was purchased in Kowloon Tong for rebuilding the Alliance Children Hostel. In 1954, the new chapel was completed, officially named ‚ÄúKowloon Tong Christian and Missionary Alliance Church‚Äù in 1955, with a focus on evangelism and education. By 1957, the adjacent Alliance Primary School had over 570 students, including a night school for underprivileged children. Over time, more than 20 daughter churches were established, leading to the 2022 reorganization into the ‚ÄúHong Kong Christian & Missionary Alliance Union Limited.‚Äù

                ‚Äî

                1. Hong Kong Christian General Council, 1943

                  (Cited from Chen Zhi Heng, 2009: 234-312)

                  During the Japanese occupation of Hong Kong, the Japanese military government established the Hong Kong Christian General Council (HKCGC). Pastor Wong Oi Tong served as President, with Pastor Liu Yue Sheng as Vice President, and Japanese advisor Moritaka Samejima was appointed as Consultant Pastor. The council mandated the unification of all denominations under its administration. While most churches joined, a few, such as Joyful Church and the True Jesus Church, refused due to doctrinal differences.

                  The HKCGC followed Japan‚Äôs wartime religious policy, modeled after the Japanese Religious Organizations Law, which aimed to consolidate Christianity into a single organization for control. Similar structures were implemented in occupied China, such as the North China Christian Federation and the East China Christian Alliance. The HKCGC was the only legally recognized Christian organization in Hong Kong during this period.

                  The council adapted existing structures from the Hong Kong Chinese Christian Churches Union, maintaining departments for evangelism, education, charity, and cemeteries while adding new ones for literature, church governance, and finance. Pastors‚Äô qualifications and salaries were standardized.

                  Though the HKCGC was criticized for collaboration, it provided a means for churches to survive under Japanese rule. After the war, it was dissolved, and churches reverted to their pre-war denominational structures.

                  ‚Äî

                  1. Theological Institutions Relocating to Hong Kong During the Chinese Civil War

                    (Cited from Xing Fu Zeng, 2004: 90-91, 122-124)

                    Unrest during the Chinese Civil War prompted theological institutions to relocate. Between 1947 and 1949, four seminaries moved to Hong Kong.

                    • Bethel Theological Seminary:

                      In 1946, Bethel reopened in Shanghai, but its principal, Lan Ru Xi, was persuaded to move the seminary to Hong Kong. In February 1947, the seminary reopened at its Kowloon City property, resuming classes in March with 68 students by 1949.

                      • Lutheran Theological Seminary:

                        Originally based in Shashi, Hubei, it moved to Tao Fong Shan, Hong Kong, in December 1948. Early graduates were encouraged to return to China, but by the third graduating class, most remained in Hong Kong, engaging in refugee ministry and founding 15 congregations with 2,203 members.

                        • Assemblies of God Bible School:

                          In 1949, J. E. Morrison relocated the school from Guangzhou to Hong Kong.

                          • Guangzhou Bible School:

                            Moved to Hong Kong in 1949 under Principal Liang Gui Min, accommodating 24 students, mainly from Guangdong.

                            These relocations marked a shift in Hong Kong‚Äôs role, transforming it from a temporary refuge to a center for Chinese theological education. After the establishment of the People‚Äôs Republic of China, Hong Kong became a hub for Chinese seminary training and church leadership development.

                              2. Ë´ãÂú®ÊñáÊ™îÊú´„ÄåÊñáÁçª „Äç ËôïÊ∑ªÂä†ÈóúÊñºÊØèÂÄã‰∫ã‰ª∂ÁöÑÁõ∏ÈóúÊñáÁçªÁöÑÂá∫ËôïÔºàËá≥Â∞ë‰∏ÄËôïÂá∫ËôïÔºåË∂äÂ§öË∂äÂ•Ω„ÄÇ Â¶ÇÂèØËÉΩÔºå‰πüË´ãÂåÖÊã¨‰∏≠Êó•ÈüìËã±ÊàñÂÖ∂‰ªñÊ≠êÊ¥≤Ë™ûË®ÄÁöÑÊñáÁçªÂá∫ËôïÔºâ„ÄÇ ‚òê

                            1. Ordination of Chinese Theologian Zhao Zichen in Hong Kong

                              (Cited from Xing Fu Zeng, 2003: 139-140; Wu Qing, 2017: 76-79; Paton, 1985: 103-105)

                              On July 20, 1941, Zhao Zichen, a prominent Chinese theologian and Dean of the School of Religion at Yenching University, was ordained as a pastor in a unique ceremony held in Hong Kong. The ordination was conducted at the small chapel of the Bishop‚Äôs House by Bishop R. O. Hall, who performed three separate rites: transferring Zhao into the Anglican communion, commissioning him as a deacon, and ordaining him as a priest. Originally affiliated with the Methodist Church, Zhao‚Äôs connection with the Anglican Church was influenced by his longstanding relationship with Bishop Hall.

                              In 1939, Zhao took a sabbatical from Yenching University and accepted an invitation to serve as a pastor at Wenlin Chapel in Kunming. During his time there, he inquired about joining the Anglican Church. Upon returning to Beijing, Zhao expressed that his sense of calling had become clearer. One of his students, Luo Zhenfang, recounted Zhao saying that the acceptance of church authority resolved many of his spiritual struggles. Bishop Hall recalled Zhao stating that he sought not only to ‚Äújoin the priesthood‚Äù but also to ‚Äúsubmit to the priesthood.‚Äù

                              However, Zhao faced severe accusations during the 1951 denunciation campaigns at Yenching University. In response, Bishop Ling Xianyang of the North China Diocese publicly revoked Zhao‚Äôs priestly duties and all associated responsibilities. Despite this, Zhao was invited in 1954 as a delegate to the National Christian Conference, where he was elected as a committee member.

                              ‚Äî

                              1. Publication of History of Christianity in Hong Kong, 1941

                                (Cited from Xing Fu Zeng, 2014b: 245-246)

                                The publication of History of Christianity in Hong Kong was led by Pastor Liu Yue Sheng of the Baptist Independent Church on Caine Road. Liu had served in Guangzhou and Shanghai as a Baptist minister before being invited to pastor the Chinese Baptist Church in San Francisco (1929‚Äì1932). He returned to Guangzhou in 1932, becoming pastor of East Hill Baptist Church and holding positions such as President of the Guangdong Baptist Association and the National Baptist Union of China.

                                In 1934, Liu edited A Brief History of Baptist Churches in Guangdong for the association and, in 1936, An Overview of Christianity in Guangzhou for the city‚Äôs Christian Council. After moving to Hong Kong in 1938, Liu was elected Vice President of the Hong Kong Chinese Christian Churches Union. Although the union lacked a formal publishing division, Liu spearheaded the editorial work for the History of Christianity in Hong Kong.

                                In the preface, Liu explained that in April 1940, the union decided to compile and publish a historical record. A committee of seven, including prominent figures such as Zhang Zhu Ling, Wong Oi Tong, and Liu, oversaw the project. Divided into seven sections, the book covered denominational histories, church profiles, joint ventures, cultural endeavors, charitable work, social services, and biographies. Published in August 1941, it was described as a pioneering effort in documenting Hong Kong‚Äôs Christian history.

                                ‚Äî

                                1. Anglican Church‚Äôs Ten-Year Forward Movement, 1946

                                  (Cited from Zhong Ren Li, 1951: 33)

                                  After World War II, Bishop R. O. Hall returned to Hong Kong and launched the ‚ÄúTen-Year Forward Movement‚Äù in January 1946. This initiative aimed to double the number of believers and clergy within the Hong Kong and Guangdong Diocese over a decade. Members were encouraged to bring at least one friend to church each week and pray daily for those interested in faith.

                                  The movement formally began on January 25, 1946, with a flag presentation ceremony at St. John‚Äôs Cathedral. The flags, bearing St. Andrew‚Äôs Cross, symbolized the call to evangelize modeled after St. Andrew. In The Voice of the Church in South China, Pastor Zhong Ren Li emphasized the need for comprehensive promotion, including written materials to supplement in-person advocacy.

                                  The Ten-Year Forward Movement was part of a broader post-war trend, as other denominations launched similar campaigns. For instance, the National Christian Council of China initiated a three-year ‚ÄúChristian Advance Campaign‚Äù (1947‚Äì1950) to spread the Gospel and rebuild moral foundations.

                                  ‚Äî

                                  1. Centenary of the Victoria Diocese, 1949

                                    (Cited from Zhong Ren Li, 1951: 36-39)

                                    The Victoria Diocese, established in 1849, celebrated its centenary in 1949. Over the century, the diocese underwent several territorial changes, losing regions as new dioceses were formed, including North China (1872), Japan (1883), Fujian (1906), Guangxi-Hunan (1909), and Yunnan-Guizhou (1947). By 1947, the Hong Kong and Guangdong Diocese (formerly Victoria Diocese) covered Hong Kong, Guangdong, and Guangxi.

                                    Centenary events included a fundraising campaign, a diocesan council meeting, a study retreat, and a thanksgiving service. The council elected Reverend Mu Rong Xian as the Bishop of Guangdong. The celebration also produced historical accounts, including Zhong Ren Li‚Äôs A Brief History of the Anglican Diocese of South China (1951) and The Diocese of Victoria, Hong Kong: A Hundred Years Church History, 1849‚Äì1949 by George B. Endacott and Dorothy E. She.

                                    ‚Äî

                                    1. Christian Contingency Meetings, 1949

                                      (Cited from Xing Fu Zeng, 2004: 102; He Ming Zhang, 2021: 55-65)

                                      Amid the Chinese Civil War, churches in South China prepared for Communist control. In July and August 1949, the Guangdong branch of the National Christian Council of China held a summer conference at Diocesan Boys‚Äô School in Hong Kong, known as the ‚ÄúGuangdong Christian Affairs Review Conference.‚Äù Participants from Guangdong, Hong Kong, and Macau discussed strategies for addressing Communist challenges.

                                      Churches adopted a dual approach: while emphasizing that missionary organizations would not retreat immediately, they established financial offices in Hong Kong and relocated property deeds and archives as precautions.

                                      For instance, the Christian and Missionary Alliance (C&MA) evacuated approximately 80 missionaries to Hong Kong in June 1949. Alfred C. Snead, Secretary-General of C&MA‚Äôs Foreign Missions Department, established a temporary ‚ÄúAll-China Alliance Office‚Äù in Hong Kong, which operated until 1953, when the ‚ÄúChina-Hong Kong Field‚Äù was formally created. This reflected the widespread strategic adjustments churches made to navigate the uncertain political landscape.

                                        1. Ë´ãÂú®ÊñáÊ™îÊú´„ÄåÊñáÁçª „Äç ËôïÊ∑ªÂä†ÈóúÊñºÊØèÂÄã‰∫ã‰ª∂ÁöÑÁõ∏ÈóúÊñáÁçªÁöÑÂá∫ËôïÔºàËá≥Â∞ë‰∏ÄËôïÂá∫ËôïÔºåË∂äÂ§öË∂äÂ•Ω„ÄÇ Â¶ÇÂèØËÉΩÔºå‰πüË´ãÂåÖÊã¨‰∏≠Êó•ÈüìËã±ÊàñÂÖ∂‰ªñÊ≠êÊ¥≤Ë™ûË®ÄÁöÑÊñáÁçªÂá∫ËôïÔºâ„ÄÇ ‚òê

                                      (1) Refusal to Join the Hong Kong Christian General Council

                                      (Cited from Hu En De, 2022: 73, 115-117; Xing Fu Zeng, 2014: 203-206; Chen Zhi Heng, 2009: 251-256)

                                      In 1943, under the directive of the Japanese Governor‚Äôs Office, the Hong Kong Christian General Council (HKCGC) was established to unify denominations and create a structure akin to a ‚ÄúUnited Church.‚Äù While most Christian denominations and independent churches in Hong Kong joined the council, notable exceptions included the Joyful Gospel Church and the True Jesus Church.

                                      Hu En De, leader of the Joyful Gospel Church, initially attended the HKCGC‚Äôs inaugural ceremony and was undecided about joining. Some urged him to participate, including Vice President Liu Yue Sheng, who warned, ‚ÄúYou must not take the Japanese lightly; they are difficult to deal with.‚Äù Within the Joyful Gospel Church, members debated the risks of refusing membership. However, a foreign believer tearfully warned Hu against joining, describing the council as a ‚Äúcompromised group of liberal Christians.‚Äù This pushed Hu to read The Light of Scripture one evening, where he encountered the verse, ‚ÄúDo not be yoked together with unbelievers‚Äù (2 Corinthians 6:14). He resolved not to join the council, reasoning, ‚ÄúHow can we treat liberal Christians as members of Christ‚Äôs body? They do not believe in salvation, the truth of Christ‚Äôs atonement through His blood, or His divinity. With this in mind, I decided not to join.‚Äù

                                      Hu‚Äôs decision reflected the Fundamentalist resistance to ecumenism, particularly with liberal theological traditions. It highlighted the broader conflict between Fundamentalism and Liberalism in the 20th century.

                                      In July 1944, at an HKCGC executive committee meeting, Japanese advisor Moritaka Samejima proposed a final attempt to persuade non-member churches to join. However, no resolution was reached.

                                      During this period, Hu was briefly detained by the Japanese military police, who suspected him of signaling enemies with a flashlight from a rooftop. After questioning, he was released. The Joyful Gospel Church adjusted its services, holding meetings during the day instead of evenings. Evangelistic activities ceased, relying solely on personal evangelism, and attendance declined.

                                      (2) Controversy Over Ordination of Women Priests in the Anglican Church (1944)

                                      (Cited from Li Tim Oi, 1993; Wu Qing, 2015: 95-110; Paton, 1985: 127-148; Weikl, 2018: 100-131)

                                      On January 25, 1944, Florence Li Tim Oi (1907‚Äì1992) became the first female priest in the worldwide Anglican Communion, ordained by Bishop R. O. Hall during wartime pastoral needs. This unprecedented event sparked significant controversy and temporarily concluded when Li resigned her priestly status in early 1946.

                                      Born in Hong Kong, Li Tim Oi dedicated herself to ministry in 1931. After graduating from Union Theological Seminary in Guangzhou in 1937, she served as an assistant preacher at All Saints Church in Kowloon before transferring to St. Mark‚Äôs Church in Macau in 1940 to oversee refugee relief efforts. With Hong Kong‚Äôs fall in 1941, the influx of refugees to Macau grew. In May 1941, Bishop Hall ordained Li as a deaconess. By 1942, with wartime challenges preventing priests from administering the Eucharist in Macau, Bishop Mok Shou Tsang permitted Li to officiate the service.

                                      In 1943, Bishop Hall informed Archbishop William Temple of his intention to ordain Li as a priest, emphasizing the pastoral necessity. Temple advised that such decisions should be handled by the Chung Hua Sheng Kung Hui (CHSKH, the Chinese Anglican Church) and expressed reservations about the broader implications. Despite this, Hall proceeded with the ordination in Zhaoqing in January 1944 and later informed Temple, who responded without taking a definitive stance.

                                      The ordination became public in mid-1944, igniting widespread debate in the British church. In November 1944, Temple‚Äôs successor, Archbishop Geoffrey Fisher, deemed the ordination ‚Äúultra vires‚Äù (beyond legal authority) and warned of potential Anglican schism. Fisher urged Hall to revoke Li‚Äôs priestly status, but Hall argued that while he might have overstepped ecclesiastical norms, he had not violated spiritual principles.

                                      In March 1946, the CHSKH convened its first post-war synod and expressed regret over the ordination. Before the meeting, Li learned that Hall faced a choice between resigning as bishop or having her relinquish her priesthood. To protect Hall, Li voluntarily resigned.

                                      Despite her resignation, the issue persisted. In May 1946, the Hong Kong and South China Diocese proposed allowing women‚Äôs ordination. This forced the CHSKH‚Äôs 10th General Assembly in 1948 to address the proposal. While Bishop Hall emphasized its pastoral significance, the assembly deferred the matter to the Lambeth Conference. Reverend Zhong Ren Li later criticized the CHSKH for lacking ‚Äúmoral courage‚Äù and missing a significant opportunity.

                                      The Anglican Church in Hong Kong continued advocating for women‚Äôs ordination, culminating in Bishop Gilbert Baker ordaining Joyce Bennett and Jane Hwang as the first Anglican female priests in 1971. The Church of England only approved women‚Äôs ordination in 1992 and female bishops in 2014.

                                      After the establishment of the People‚Äôs Republic of China, Li Tim Oi remained in the mainland. During the 1960s, under political pressure, she publicly denounced Bishop Hall as an imperialist spy and described her ordination as a ploy to undermine Chinese patriotism. In 1982, she moved to Canada and attended the Lambeth Conference in 1988. She passed away in 1992.

                                      During this decade, the main opposition to and impact on Christianity primarily took place during the Japanese occupation period. Please see the text below for further details.

                                      Ê≠§ÂçÅÂπ¥‰∏ªË¶ÅÂèçÂ∞çÂèäË°ùÊìäÂü∫Áù£ÊïôËÄÖÔºå‰∏ªË¶ÅÊòØÊó•Ê≤ªÊ∑™Èô∑ÊôÇÊúüÔºåÂèÉ‰∏ãÊñá„ÄÇ

                                      ÂèçÂü∫Áù£ÊïôÁöÑ‰∫ã‰ª∂/ÈÅãÂãïÔºàÂåÖÊã¨ÈáùÂ∞çÂü∫Áù£ÊïôÁöÑÁ´∂Áà≠‰∫ã‰ª∂ÂíåÈÅãÂãïÔºâ

                                      1. Ë´ãÂú®‰∏ãÊñπ „ÄåÊÇ®ÁöÑÂõûÁ≠î„Äç ÈÉ®ÂàÜÊèèËø∞ÈÄôÂçÅÂπ¥ÈñìÈáçË¶ÅÁöÑÂèçÂ∞çÂü∫Áù£ÊïôÁöÑ‰∫ã‰ª∂ÊàñÈÅãÂãï„ÄÇ ÈÄô‰∫õÈÅãÂãïÂåÖÊã¨ÈáùÂ∞çÊïôÊúÉÊàñËÄÖÂÄã‰∫∫ÁöÑÈõÜÊúÉÔºåÊîøÂ∫úÈÄÆÊçï/Ëµ∑Ë®¥Âü∫Áù£ÂæíÁöÑË°åÁÇ∫Ôºå‰ª•ÂèäÊ∞ëÁúæÊö¥ÂäõÊîªÊìä‰∫ã‰ª∂Á≠â„ÄÇ

                                        1. Ë´ãÂàóÂá∫ÈÄôÂçÅÂπ¥ÈñìËá≥Â∞ë‰∏Ä‰ª∂ÔºåËá≥Â§ö‰∫î‰ª∂Á§æÊúÉ‰∫ã‰ª∂ÊàñÈÅãÂãï

                                          1. Ë´ãÁõ°ÂèØËÉΩÂàóËàâÊï∏ÈáèË≥áË®äÂíåÊï∏Êìö„ÄÇ ‚òê

                                          2. Ë´ãÂú®ÊñáÊ™îÊú´„ÄåÊñáÁçª „ÄçËôïÊ∑ªÂä†ÈóúÊñºÊØèÂÄã‰∫ã‰ª∂ÁöÑÁõ∏ÈóúÊñáÁçªÁöÑÂá∫ËôïÔºàËá≥Â∞ë‰∏ÄËôïÂá∫ËôïÔºåË∂äÂ§öË∂äÂ•Ω„ÄÇ Â¶ÇÂèØËÉΩÔºå‰πüË´ãÂåÖÊã¨‰∏≠Êó•ÈüìËã±ÊàñÂÖ∂‰ªñÊ≠êÊ¥≤Ë™ûË®ÄÁöÑÊñáÁçªÂá∫ËôïÔºâ„ÄÇ ‚òê


                                        ÊÇ®ÁöÑÂõûÁ≠îÔºö

                                        (1) The St. Stephen‚Äôs College Massacre (Banham 2003: 248-276; Chen Tianquan 2024: 100-103)

                                        St. Stephen‚Äôs College was founded in 1903 by the Anglican Church in Sai Ying Pun. In 1928, the school relocated to Stanley, with its new campus situated on the site of the former Stanley Barracks. During the Battle of Hong Kong and the subsequent occupation, St. Stephen‚Äôs College became a significant chapter in this tumultuous history.

                                        On December 22, British forces retreated to Stanley, fighting bitterly until December 25, when the defensive line fell. In the early hours of Christmas Day, Japanese forces stormed St. Stephen‚Äôs College. Despite the building displaying the Red Cross flag, the Japanese troops massacred over 60 wounded soldiers and medical staff inside. Female nurses were subjected to horrific assaults, including rape. Among the victims was Tan Cheung-Hsuen, the Head of the Chinese Language Department, an alumnus of the school who had enrolled at the University of Hong Kong in 1918, attended the World‚Äôs Student Christian Federation Conference in Beijing in 1922, and began teaching at St. Stephen‚Äôs in

  3. Remaining at the school during the war, he was brutally killed at the age of 41. His body was initially buried in Stanley Village and later reinterred in the Stanley Military Cemetery after the war.

    (2) The Reverend Chang Chu-Ling ‚ÄúSpy‚Äù Case (Chen Zhiheng 1999: 208-223)

    Reverend Chang Chu-Ling was the senior pastor of the Union Church of the Chinese Christian Church. From May 1942 to March 1945, Japanese military police conducted 15 investigations into the Union Church, focusing on its history, financial assets, congregation size, and leadership. On October 21, 1943, a secret agent summoned Reverend Chang to the Western District Gendarmerie for questioning. Though this was not his first summons, this time he was detained and accused of being a spy for the Chongqing Nationalist Government.

    During his 19-day imprisonment, Chang was interrogated 11 times and subjected to severe torture, including beatings, being suspended upside down, whipped with thick belts, and deprived of water for over 30 hours. Facing such torment, he relied on prayer for strength.

    After Reverend Chang‚Äôs disappearance, the General Assembly‚Äôs advisor, Pastor Sawajima, sought his whereabouts and negotiated with Colonel Noma, the head of the Japanese military police. Chang later recounted that he was suspected of being an Anglo-American spy due to his fiery sermons, criticism of Japan‚Äôs hostile mindset, and frequent preaching of the gospel of peace, which was perceived as opposition to the ‚Äúholy war.‚Äù He was also suspected of assisting people in returning to their hometowns. Even after his release, the Union Church remained under investigation. Chang required over 20 days of treatment to recover, though his hands, damaged from hanging, left him unable to fully extend his fingers. This pain persisted for years, particularly before storms.

    At the time of his imprisonment, Reverend Chang was 66 years old. The ordeal inflicted lasting physical and mental trauma. He retired from the Union Church in September 1950 but continued to serve as an advisory pastor until his passing in 1961.

    (3) Christianity in Detention Camps (Ho Wing-Kei 2111: 179-194; Chen Zhiheng 1999: 313-338; Fetzer & Soper 2015)

    During the Japanese occupation of Hong Kong, the Japanese army detained over 10,000 foreign nationals from ‚Äúenemy countries‚Äù in various camps. Foreign Christians in Hong Kong were no exception. For example, the Stanley Internment Camp, located in the southern part of Hong Kong Island, housed approximately 2,400 British nationals, 300 Americans, and over 60 Dutch nationals. Among them were 40 pastors and missionaries from over 20 denominations, including the Anglican Church (from Britain, Ireland, Scotland, and the U.S.), Presbyterian Church (from Ireland, New Zealand, and the U.S.), Southern Baptist Convention, Salvation Army, Methodist Church, Lutheran Church, Seventh-Day Adventists, Assemblies of God, Christian and Missionary Alliance, Congregational Church, Religious Society of Friends, and Roman Catholic Church.

    In his memoir Too Hot for Comfort, British Methodist missionary Bill Ream recalled religious life in the Stanley Internment Camp. On January 25, 1942, the first Sunday worship service was held in the college hall. The morning and afternoon services were attended by over 300 and 100-200 people, respectively. In these harsh conditions, believers of various traditions united, with Free Church pastors assisting in Anglican Eucharist ceremonies and sermons delivered by pastors from different denominations. In addition to worship, the camp offered Sunday school for children and adults, later expanding to include youth services and teacher training programs. Morning prayers were held daily at 8:30 a.m., alternating between Anglican, Free Church, and litany prayers.

    Special gatherings were held on Christmas, Good Friday, and Easter. In September 1943, the camp hosted a week-long gospel campaign themed ‚ÄúQuo Vadis?‚Äù (‚ÄúWhere are you going?‚Äù). Bill Ream noted that, at the time, American and Canadian detainees were gradually repatriated, leaving those still interned uncertain about their future, making the theme especially poignant. In August 1944, a blackout prompted spontaneous gatherings in the camp, where believers shared scriptures and prayed together in the dark.

    However, unity was occasionally challenged. In April 1942, some American left-wing evangelicals insisted on holding separate services, which Ream described as a ‚Äútraumatic experience.‚Äù While the religious services in the internment camp brought together different traditions, they did not constitute a formal ‚ÄúUnited Church‚Äù but rather ‚ÄúUnited Congregations.‚Äù

    The altar for the joint worship services featured a golden cross that had once adorned the entrance of Mount Gough Peak Protestant Church (built in 1883), which was destroyed during the Battle of Hong Kong. Pastors carried the cross to the Stanley Internment Camp, and after the war, it was relocated to St. John‚Äôs Cathedral. In September 1958, on Michaelmas, the cross was presented to St. Stephen‚Äôs Church in Stanley, with an inscription commemorating its history.

    Additionally, in a chapel at the Sham Shui Po Prisoner-of-War Camp, detainees created an altar using a box lid and a cross salvaged from a garbage heap. The altar bore the prayer of St. Richard of Chichester:

    ‚ÄúThanks be to my Lord Jesus Christ, for all the blessings Thou hast given me,
    For all the pains and insults Thou hast borne for me.
    O most merciful Redeemer, Friend, and Brother,
    May I know Thee more clearly, love Thee more dearly, and follow Thee more nearly, day by day. Amen.‚Äù

    After the war, the altar was moved to the Chapel of St. Michael the Archangel in St. John‚Äôs Cathedral.

Christian or Pro-Christian Events/Movements

  1. Wong Oi-tong (ÁéãÊÑõÊ£†, 1881‚Äì1957) (Cited from Li Zhigang, 2014: 31-35)

    Wong Oi-tong, born in Dongguan, Guangdong, was a third-generation Christian. His grandfather, Wong Chun-kwong, and father, Wong Siu-tuen, were both members and preachers of the Rhenish Mission. Wong Oi-tong studied at the Rhenish Primary School in Fuyong, Bao‚Äôan County, before attending Rhenish Secondary School in Yeshan, Tangxia, Dongguan. At the age of 20, he entered the Rhenish Theological Seminary in Dongguan and graduated at 22. The following year, he was ordained as a preacher for the Rhenish Mission. After arriving in Hong Kong in 1909, he remained there permanently.

    In 1914, when the Rhenish Church in Hong Kong was established, Wong assisted Pastor Chan Koon-hai in managing church affairs. During World War I, due to hostilities between Britain and Germany, German missionaries in Hong Kong were ordered to leave. As the Rhenish Mission had German roots, it was placed under the custody of the London Missionary Society. Wong drafted and established the church‚Äôs regulations during this period. In 1917, he was ordained as a pastor in Humen. By 1925, after the London Missionary Society completed its custodianship, the Rhenish Mission achieved self-governance. In 1926, Pastor Yip To-sheng arrived to take over church administration, and Wong was elected Vice President of the General Assembly.

    Wong was also a founding member of the Hong Kong Christian Council in 1914, serving as its first secretary and drafting its constitution. He held various roles, such as head of the Council‚Äôs Evangelism and Public Cemetery Departments. Wong actively opposed the practice of keeping slave girls, publishing articles in The Great Light Newspaper to condemn this custom. During the Japanese occupation of Hong Kong in 1941, Wong was appointed President of the Japanese-controlled Hong Kong Christian Council due to the Rhenish Mission‚Äôs German background. After the war, the Council was disbanded, and its departments resumed their activities. Wong suffered a stroke in December 1948, leaving him paralyzed, and he passed away in May 1957.

    1. Moritaka Samejima (ÈÆ´Â≥∂ÁõõÈöÜ, 1897‚Äì1986)

      (Cited from Chen Zhiheng, 2009: 234-246; Samejima Moritaka, 1971)

      Moritaka Samejima was born in December 1897 in Sasebo, Nagasaki. After graduating from the Kansai Gakuin Theological Department in 1927, he pursued further studies at Emory University and the San Francisco Theological Seminary. In 1931, he was ordained as a pastor at the Kobe Hirano Methodist Church and became the district superintendent of the Korean Methodist Church in Seoul in 1934. In 1941, he was appointed Chaplain (later Religious Director) at Kansai University.

      In September 1943, Samejima was summoned to Hong Kong to serve as an advisor to the Japanese-controlled ‚ÄúHong Kong Christian Council,‚Äù a position he held until the war‚Äôs end. His primary duty was to act as a liaison between the Japanese governor‚Äôs office and the Christian churches. For instance, upon learning that the Japanese military intended to remove the stained-glass windows of St. John‚Äôs Cathedral, Samejima intervened to ensure their preservation. He also opposed the conversion of churches into stables and worked to secure the release of Reverend Chang Chu-ling, who was falsely accused of espionage by the Japanese military.

      After Japan‚Äôs surrender, Samejima was interned and later repatriated to Japan. In 1946, he returned to Kansai Gakuin as Dean of Student Affairs and served as the eighth Chaplain from November 1946 to June

  1. From 1951 to 1974, he was the President of Chinzei Gakuin. Samejima revisited Hong Kong in 1969, where he was warmly received by the local church community. In 1970, he published Recollections of Hong Kong: The Church Under Occupation, which was translated into Chinese the following year. He passed away in February 1986.

    1. Yan-Tak Woo (ËÉ°ÊÅ©Âæ∑, 1907‚Äì2005)

      (Cited from Xing Fuzeng, 2014a: 203-206)

      Yan-Tak Woo was born in May 1907 into a prominent Christian family in Hong Kong. His grandfather, Woo Him-hang (1830‚Äì1889), was a pastor in the Berlin Mission, while his father, Woo Yee-tung (1884‚Äì1948), served as a deacon at St. Stephen‚Äôs Church under the Anglican Church. Yan-Tak Woo was the second of eight children. He was baptized at St. Stephen‚Äôs Church at birth. His mother, Wong Pik-lin (1885‚Äì1965), was the seventh daughter of Pastor Wong Chim-yu (1847‚Äì1907), a minister in the Rhenish Mission and a member of the Chinese Evangelistic Society founded by Karl G√ºtzlaff.

      Woo attended Ying Wa Girls‚Äô School and a sports school in Central before enrolling at Diocesan Boys‚Äô School. In 1926, while preparing for the University of Hong Kong‚Äôs entrance exam, he was diagnosed with tuberculosis and moved to his cousin‚Äôs clinic in Aberdeen to recover. During this period, he attended the first Believers‚Äô Bible Conference in 1927, where he was deeply influenced by Pastor Wong Yuen-so (Christian and Missionary Alliance in Guangxi). Woo experienced a profound spiritual awakening, leading to his active ministry.

      From 1930 to 1935, Woo assumed full-time ministry responsibilities at the Joyful Church, initially established by Julia Meadows, a faith missionary. He continued leading the church through its relocations and name changes, eventually settling in Tai Po Road under the name Joyful Gospel Church by 1937.

      During the Japanese occupation of Hong Kong in 1941, Woo refused to join the Japanese-controlled Christian Council, citing ‚Äúdifferences in faith.‚Äù As a result, he faced pressure and was briefly detained by the Japanese military police on suspicions of collaborating with Western powers.

      After the war, Woo was invited to lead revival meetings across China and became a prominent preacher, especially influential in student fellowship movements. His ministry and the Joyful Gospel Church became significant references for the evangelical movement in Hong Kong. Woo later served as the principal of seminaries in Singapore, the Philippines, and Hong Kong. He authored numerous works, including Running Toward the Goal, Table Devotions, and The Christian and the Sabbath. Woo passed away in 2005 after a long illness.

      1. John So (Ëòá‰ΩêÊèö, 1916‚Äì2007)

        (Cited from Li Jinqiang, 2014: 211-213)

        John So was born in 1916 in Hong Kong to a Christian family from Yangjiang County, Guangdong Province. A third-generation Christian, So was passionate about music and literature from an early age, composing over 600 Hymns of Heaven and Earth and more than 200 Sacred Songs of Heaven and Earth throughout his life. In 1931, he committed to serving God after attending a revival meeting in Kowloon led by Chinese evangelist Song Shangjie (John Sung). In 1934, So went to the North China Theological Seminary in Teng County, Shandong Province, for theological education. After graduating in 1937, he engaged in rural evangelism in Zhangjiakou, Chahar, and Shanxi before returning to Hong Kong.

        In 1940, So was invited to assist in editing a Hebrew dictionary and teaching music at the North China Theological Seminary. In 1941, Pastor Yang Shaotang invited him to teach Hebrew in Tianjin‚Äôs Spiritual Work League. Later, he returned to Hong Kong to teach biblical studies at the Bethel Theological Seminary, which had relocated from Shanghai. During the Japanese occupation of Hong Kong, So fled to Zhangzhou, Fujian, where he founded the Heaven and Earth Daily. In 1943, he joined the faculty at Guizhou Bethel Theological Seminary, where he also launched The Gospel Magazine for the Miao Christian community. He later taught at the China Spiritual Theology Seminary in Chongqing, working alongside Pastor Jia Yuming.

        In 1945, So served as the General Secretary of the ‚ÄúGansu-Ningxia-Qinghai Christian Federation,‚Äù promoting inter-church initiatives in Northwest China. He was ordained as a pastor in Gansu in 1947 and later worked in Shanghai with the China Inland Mission on literature ministry before returning to Hong Kong in 1948.

        Between 1953 and 1961, So embarked on an eight-year evangelistic journey across East Asia, preaching in 13 countries, including Japan, Singapore, Malaysia, Australia, and Indonesia. From 1962 to 1976, he undertook global evangelistic tours across six continents and more than 50 countries. In 1976, after returning from the Americas, he became president of the ‚ÄúGlobal Evangelistic Association‚Äù and founded the ‚ÄúChristian Heaven and Earth Society,‚Äù publishing over 50 books, including Bible Difficulties (10 volumes), New Testament Meditations (8 volumes), and Old Testament Insights (3 volumes).

        He passed away in September 2007 at the age of 91.

        ‚Äî

        1. John K. C. Ma (È¶¨Êï¨ÂÖ®, 1917‚Äì2011)

          (Cited from Congregational Church, 2012; Peng Shumin, 2014: 92-95)

          John K. C. Ma was born in 1917 at the site of the Hong Kong Congregational Church. As a teenager, he accompanied his father, Pastor Ma Yaodong, in street evangelism, playing the accordion and leading hymns. After graduating from Wah Yan College in 1935, he worked at the Salvation Army in Guangzhou. During World War II, he served as a member of the International Relief Council. In 1944, Ma graduated from the Union Theological Seminary in Guangzhou and became the pastor of the Qujiang Chapel of the Chinese YMCA in 1945. The following year, he was appointed Youth Secretary of the Guangdong Association of the Chinese Christian Church and was ordained as a pastor at Renji Chapel in Guangzhou in 1946.

          In 1947, Ma attended the World Christian Youth Conference in Oslo, where he reaffirmed his lifelong call to ministry. After the conference, during a stopover in Hong Kong, he learned of the Congregational Church‚Äôs urgent need for leadership. In September 1947, Ma decided to stay and became the second senior pastor of the Congregational Church, serving for 40 years until his retirement in 1987, except for a year of advanced study at Union Theological Seminary in New York (1956‚Äì1957).

          During his tenure, Ma initiated the construction of new church facilities. To fund the Leighton Road Chapel, he proposed issuing bonds with a 5% annual interest, a plan that successfully raised the required funds within a year. The chapel, initially called ‚ÄúCaroline Hill Road Chapel,‚Äù was dedicated in 1950. Later, Ma led the redevelopment of the Ladder Street church building, completed in 1971 and renamed ‚ÄúBridges Street Chapel.‚Äù

          Ma also taught at theological institutions, including the Hong Kong Theological Seminary, Chung Chi Divinity School, and the Lutheran Theological Seminary. He served as chairman of the Hong Kong Council of the Church of Christ in China and as vice-chairman of the Hong Kong Chinese Christian Churches Union. Ma suggested limiting the union board members‚Äô terms to six years with a mandatory one-year break after.

          He passed away in April 2011 at the age of 94.

          ‚Äî

          1. Denial Cheung (ÂºµÊúâÂÖâ, 1917‚Äì1996)

            (Cited from Wong Cailian, 2014: 42-46)

            Denial Cheung was born in 1917 in Gaoming, Guangdong Province, into a pastor‚Äôs family. His father, Cheung Shing-yau, was a minister of the Holiness Church. In 1927, after moving to Hong Kong, Cheung was baptized at the Kowloon Union Church and became active in Sunday School, youth fellowship, and the choir. He experienced a spiritual revival during a John Sung evangelistic campaign in Hong Kong while in high school. After graduating from Munsang College in 1937, Cheung worked at the Far Eastern Missionary Society‚Äôs seminary in Guangzhou as an English translator and music teacher.

            In 1940, Cheung entered Bethel Theological Seminary, which had relocated to Hong Kong from Shanghai. During the Japanese occupation, he followed the seminary to Guangxi and Guizhou, graduating in 1943. He ministered in Guizhou and joined evangelist Ji Zhiwen on preaching tours.

            After the war, Cheung moved back to Hong Kong and served as a religious officer for the YMCA. Later, he worked at Kowloon City Baptist Church, initially as a volunteer director of youth and choir ministries, before being ordained as a pastor in 1949. He pursued advanced studies in religious education at Southwestern Baptist Theological Seminary in the U.S. in 1952 and returned to Kowloon City Baptist Church in 1954. He led significant reforms in Sunday School, doubling participation between 1954 and 1959.

            In 1959, Cheung emigrated to the U.S., working with the Chinese Consolidated Benevolent Association in Chicago. He earned a master‚Äôs degree in religious education in 1965 and returned to Hong Kong later that year, resuming his role at Kowloon City Baptist Church. Cheung served as chairman or president of various organizations, including the Hong Kong Chinese Christian Churches Union, the Hong Kong Baptist Convention, and the Baptist World Alliance. He chaired the Billy Graham Crusades in Hong Kong in 1956 and 1976.

            Cheung retired in 1980 and passed away in 1996 at the age of 79.

            Ëòá‰ΩêÊèöÔºà1916-2007ÔºâÔºåÂª£Êù±ÁúÅÈôΩÊ±üÁ∏£‰∫∫ÔºåÁîüÊñºÈ¶ôÊ∏ØÔºå‰ºØÁ¨¨‰∏â‰ª£Âü∫Áù£Âæí„ÄÇËá™ÂπºÂñúÊÑõÈü≥Ê®ÇÂíåÊñáÂ≠∏Ôºå‰∏ÄÁîüÂâµ‰Ωú‰∫Ü„ÄäÂ§©‰∫∫Áü≠Ê≠å„Äã600‰ΩôÈ¶ñÂèä„ÄäÂ§©‰∫∫ËÅñÊ≠å„Äã200È§òÈ¶ñ„ÄÇ1931Âπ¥Ôºå‰∏≠ÂúãÊïôÊúÉËëóÂêç‰ΩàÈÅìÂÆ∂ÂÆãÂ∞öÁØÄ‰æÜÊ∏Ø‰ΩàÈÅìÔºåËòá‰ΩêÊèöÂú®‰πùÈæç‰æø‰ª•Âà©ÊúÉÁöÑÂüπÈùà‰ΩàÈÅìÊúÉ‰∏≠Ê±∫ÂøóÁµÇË∫´‰∫ãÂ•â‰∏ª„ÄÇ1934Âπ¥ÔºåÂåó‰∏äÂà∞Â±±Êù±ÁúÅÊªïÁ∏£ËèØÂåóÁ•ûÂ≠∏Èô¢Êé•ÂèóÁ•ûÂ≠∏ÊïôËÇ≤„ÄÇ1937Âπ¥Áï¢Ê•≠ÂæåÔºåÂà∞ÂØüÂìàÁàæÂºµÂÆ∂Âè£ÂèäÂ±±Ë•øÈÑâÊùëÂüéÈéÆ‰ΩàÈÅìÂæåÔºåÂõûÂà∞È¶ôÊ∏Ø„ÄÇ1940Âπ¥ÔºåÊáâÈÇÄËèØÂåóÁ•ûÂ≠∏Èô¢ÂçîÂä©Á∑®Ë≠ØÂ∏å‰ºØ‰æÜÊñáÂ≠óÂÖ∏ÂèäÊïôÊéàÈü≥Ê®Ç„ÄÇ1941Âπ¥ÔºåÊáâÊ•äÁ¥πÂîêÁâßÂ∏´ÈÇÄË´ãÂà∞Â§©Ê¥•ÁöÑÈùàÂ∑•ÂúòÊïôÊéàÂ∏å‰ºØ‰æÜÊñá„ÄÇÂæåÂõûÂà∞È¶ôÊ∏ØÔºå‰∏äÊµ∑ÈÅ∑Ê∏ØÁöÑ‰ºØÁâπÂà©Á•ûÂ≠∏Èô¢ÊïôÊéàËÅñÁ∂ìË™≤„ÄÇÈ¶ôÊ∏ØÊ∑™Èô∑ÂæåÈõ¢Ê∏ØÈÅøÈõ£ÔºåÂú®Á¶èÂª∫Êº≥Â∑ûÂâµÁ´ã„ÄäÂ§©‰∫∫Â†±„Äã„ÄÇ1943Âπ¥ÔºåÂâçÂæÄË≤¥Â∑û‰ºØÁâπÂà©Á•ûÂ≠∏Èô¢‰ªªÊïôÔºå‰∏¶Âú®Ë©≤ËôïÁÇ∫ËãóÊóèÂü∫Áù£ÂæíÂâµËæ¶‰∫ÜÂ§ßËä±ËãóÊñáÁöÑ„ÄäÁ¶èÈü≥ÈõúË™å„Äã„ÄÇ1944Âπ¥ÔºåËΩâÂà∞ÈáçÊÖ∂‰∏≠ÂúãÈùà‰øÆÁ•ûÂ≠∏Èô¢‰ªªÊïôÔºåËàáË≥àÁéâÈäòÁâßÂ∏´ÂêåÂ∑•„ÄÇ1945Âπ¥Â§èÔºå‰ªñÈÅ†Ëµ¥Ë•øÂåóÔºåÂá∫‰ªª„ÄåÁîòÂØßÈùíÂü∫Áù£ÊïôËÅØÂêàÊúÉ„ÄçÁ∏ΩÂππ‰∫ãÔºåË≤†Ë≤¨Âú®Ë•øÂåó‰∏ÄÂ∏∂Â∑°Ë¶ñÊïôÊúÉÔºåÊé®ÈÄ≤ÂêÑÁ®ÆËÅØÂêà‰∫ãÂ∑•„ÄÇ1947Âπ¥6ÊúàÔºåËòá‰ΩêÊèöÂú®ÁîòËÇÖË¢´ÊåâÁ´ãÁÇ∫ÁâßÂ∏´ÔºåÂæåÂà∞‰∏äÊµ∑ÂçîÂä©ÂÖßÂú∞ÊúÉÈñãÊãìÊñáÂ≠ó‰ΩàÈÅìÂ∑•‰ΩúÔºå1948Âπ¥ÂõûÊ∏Ø„ÄÇ

Key Theological Issues

  1. Wong Oi-tong (ÁéãÊÑõÊ£†, 1881‚Äì1957) (Cited from Li Zhigang, 2014: 31-35)

    Wong Oi-tong, born in Dongguan, Guangdong, was a third-generation Christian. His grandfather, Wong Chun-kwong, and father, Wong Siu-tuen, were both members and preachers of the Rhenish Mission. Wong Oi-tong studied at the Rhenish Primary School in Fuyong, Bao‚Äôan County, before attending Rhenish Secondary School in Yeshan, Tangxia, Dongguan. At the age of 20, he entered the Rhenish Theological Seminary in Dongguan and graduated at 22. The following year, he was ordained as a preacher for the Rhenish Mission. After arriving in Hong Kong in 1909, he remained there permanently.

    In 1914, when the Rhenish Church in Hong Kong was established, Wong assisted Pastor Chan Koon-hai in managing church affairs. During World War I, due to hostilities between Britain and Germany, German missionaries in Hong Kong were ordered to leave. As the Rhenish Mission had German roots, it was placed under the custody of the London Missionary Society. Wong drafted and established the church‚Äôs regulations during this period. In 1917, he was ordained as a pastor in Humen. By 1925, after the London Missionary Society completed its custodianship, the Rhenish Mission achieved self-governance. In 1926, Pastor Yip To-sheng arrived to take over church administration, and Wong was elected Vice President of the General Assembly.

    Wong was also a founding member of the Hong Kong Christian Council in 1914, serving as its first secretary and drafting its constitution. He held various roles, such as head of the Council‚Äôs Evangelism and Public Cemetery Departments. Wong actively opposed the practice of keeping slave girls, publishing articles in The Great Light Newspaper to condemn this custom. During the Japanese occupation of Hong Kong in 1941, Wong was appointed President of the Japanese-controlled Hong Kong Christian Council due to the Rhenish Mission‚Äôs German background. After the war, the Council was disbanded, and its departments resumed their activities. Wong suffered a stroke in December 1948, leaving him paralyzed, and he passed away in May 1957.

    ‚Äî

    1. Moritaka Samejima (ÈÆ´Â≥∂ÁõõÈöÜ, 1897‚Äì1986)

      (Cited from Chen Zhiheng, 2009: 234-246; Samejima Moritaka, 1971)

      Moritaka Samejima was born in December 1897 in Sasebo, Nagasaki. After graduating from the Kansai Gakuin Theological Department in 1927, he pursued further studies at Emory University and the San Francisco Theological Seminary. In 1931, he was ordained as a pastor at the Kobe Hirano Methodist Church and became the district superintendent of the Korean Methodist Church in Seoul in 1934. In 1941, he was appointed Chaplain (later Religious Director) at Kansai University.

      In September 1943, Samejima was summoned to Hong Kong to serve as an advisor to the Japanese-controlled ‚ÄúHong Kong Christian Council,‚Äù a position he held until the war‚Äôs end. His primary duty was to act as a liaison between the Japanese governor‚Äôs office and the Christian churches. For instance, upon learning that the Japanese military intended to remove the stained-glass windows of St. John‚Äôs Cathedral, Samejima intervened to ensure their preservation. He also opposed the conversion of churches into stables and worked to secure the release of Reverend Chang Chu-ling, who was falsely accused of espionage by the Japanese military.

      After Japan‚Äôs surrender, Samejima was interned and later repatriated to Japan. In 1946, he returned to Kansai Gakuin as Dean of Student Affairs and served as the eighth Chaplain from November 1946 to June

  1. From 1951 to 1974, he was the President of Chinzei Gakuin. Samejima revisited Hong Kong in 1969, where he was warmly received by the local church community. In 1970, he published Recollections of Hong Kong: The Church Under Occupation, which was translated into Chinese the following year. He passed away in February 1986.

    ‚Äî

    1. Yan-Tak Woo (ËÉ°ÊÅ©Âæ∑, 1907‚Äì2005)

      (Cited from Xing Fuzeng, 2014a: 203-206)

      Yan-Tak Woo was born in May 1907 into a prominent Christian family in Hong Kong. His grandfather, Woo Him-hang (1830‚Äì1889), was a pastor in the Berlin Mission, while his father, Woo Yee-tung (1884‚Äì1948), served as a deacon at St. Stephen‚Äôs Church under the Anglican Church. Yan-Tak Woo was the second of eight children. He was baptized at St. Stephen‚Äôs Church at birth. His mother, Wong Pik-lin (1885‚Äì1965), was the seventh daughter of Pastor Wong Chim-yu (1847‚Äì1907), a minister in the Rhenish Mission and a member of the Chinese Evangelistic Society founded by Karl G√ºtzlaff.

      Woo attended Ying Wa Girls‚Äô School and a sports school in Central before enrolling at Diocesan Boys‚Äô School. In 1926, while preparing for the University of Hong Kong‚Äôs entrance exam, he was diagnosed with tuberculosis and moved to his cousin‚Äôs clinic in Aberdeen to recover. During this period, he attended the first Believers‚Äô Bible Conference in 1927, where he was deeply influenced by Pastor Wong Yuen-so (Christian and Missionary Alliance in Guangxi). Woo experienced a profound spiritual awakening, leading to his active ministry.

      From 1930 to 1935, Woo assumed full-time ministry responsibilities at the Joyful Church, initially established by Julia Meadows, a faith missionary. He continued leading the church through its relocations and name changes, eventually settling in Tai Po Road under the name Joyful Gospel Church by 1937.

      During the Japanese occupation of Hong Kong in 1941, Woo refused to join the Japanese-controlled Christian Council, citing ‚Äúdifferences in faith.‚Äù As a result, he faced pressure and was briefly detained by the Japanese military police on suspicions of collaborating with Western powers.

      After the war, Woo was invited to lead revival meetings across China and became a prominent preacher, especially influential in student fellowship movements. His ministry and the Joyful Gospel Church became significant references for the evangelical movement in Hong Kong. Woo later served as the principal of seminaries in Singapore, the Philippines, and Hong Kong. He authored numerous works, including Running Toward the Goal, Table Devotions, and The Christian and the Sabbath. Woo passed away in 2005 after a long illness.
